RATING: 5
I did enjoy the pace of the game (very fast despite of the overall game length). I also the distinct paths to victory and the fact that each of those gets harder and harder to work on. What I dislike are the artworks (a "no buy" for me) which are really up to the level I expect from a boardgame in my collection and I believe from the experience I had that there is a flaw in the gameplay: you need to work on the "knowledge" track early in the game. If you don't, someone may steal the victory from the other players. In addition to that, a late mistake from one player usually lead to the final VP to be gained by someone. All those points are really major issues but it gets games to look very much alike. I believe that Antike doesn't have that much replayability. Still I enjoyed the games I had so I wouldn't turn down a game... Still with a few more plays, I guess I could get bored.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
On the postive side, this can be tremendous fun.  There's a lot going on and much to think about.  You can win lots of ways, it's elegant, it's quick, it looks good, the board has two sides, the rules are well written, it can be very edgy.  It's also easy to learn, but not to master.  Further, the roundel is a clever and fun mechanism, whilst the know-hows and simple battle system add much to the game.

On the negative side, the early game and middle game are a LOT more interesting than the end game, which can come out of nowhere and be very short -  things may finish in a flash.
Also, the end is rather a "bash 'em up" feast, and like a lot of such empire games, if you're stuck in the middle it's not so good, especially if you allow the folks in the corners to make non-aggression pacts.  In fact in that respect, this game can be worse than say, SoE, CoE or Vinci.

Despite the negatives, I enjoyed this and am very keen to play again.  I think it's better than Vinci, for instance, and maybe my rating could rise to an 8, though it's limitations will ultimately stop me rating it as a 9 or 10.

RATING: 6
Antike is a racing game. If you see it as one, its quite funny and challenging. Its defenitly played better with more than less players. Great kings (1 for each 5 cities) are way too often gained if playing with too few players. Military is tempting but only worth if captured a temple or to cripple your opponents ressources. In the last case he can always expand in another direction and your efforts are mostly senseless. If you look for a fast playing easy to teach racing game, Antike is probably worth it, but has too many games which are similar in play-style but better.

RATING: 6

Playing with a full compliment of 6 gamers did not slow the game much at all. Down time is less than 5mins. 

Lots of table talk that's great for negotiation for a greater cause. 

Leaders will certainly be targeted in the final rounds much like other games. But it's not always true. Other gamers might be able to still a win from behind in 1 move. 

Overall a light wargame that's good for introduction to area control wargames to newbies.

RATING: 8
I'm extremely disappointed that Rio Grande didn't add a nice plastic insert to organize the "over 400 wooden pieces" (I didn't count, but that was the marketing copy on the box). I mean, the game was around $65 CDN! I was crazy to purchase it at this store that I know has a high markup, knowing that I could get it for cheaper online. Argh, I am now kicking myself relentlessly. I don't know why all of a sudden I'm become so superficial in my demands for pretty plastic inserts in my game boxes.  But it's a real downer for me to see a mess of pieces all jumbled together. Must be something psychological or something, this desire to organize all the game pieces into nice little compartments...
UPDATE 1: Finally got a chance to play Antike with 4 players. What Antike lacks in game box organization, it definitely makes up for it in board game design - all the important information is displayed on the board, player cards, and player aids. Once we got the game rolling, it was easy to figure out what actions can be taken. Also, I'm starting to get more comfortable with war-like games (such as Shogun). Although I played this one overly passive, I'm starting to recognize that timing is an important factor when attacking another player - I still have to work out that part, but I do realize that I started attacking too late in my first game of Antike. Something to work on! UPDATE 2: Antike has become one of my favorite 5-6 player war-like games, up there with Shogun. It is a solid game with plenty of player interaction and just enough euro-mechanics to keep me interested. With 6 gamers and 4+ hours, I'll be voting for Antike or Shogun on game night.

RATING: 7
Antike certainly deserves some attention as a civilization building game with decent spatial, wargaming, and "get the rewards early and strategically" elements.  

Antike feels as if it is somewhat related to Eclipse.  If you like Eclipse, you should probably play its predecessor, Antike, at least once.  Similar in some respects?  Totally different?  Of course, Eclipse, lacks a rondelle (thankfully), but how about the occupation of resource specific planet/countries, building resource engines through the occupation of those planet/countries, and pursuing varied goals while keeping an eye on conquering things?  Have some fun playing both...then you decide.

RATING: 7
PROS:
*) Fast moving. I believe this is the first Civ-themed game where I asked "what, my turn already?", and meant it.
*) A sense of empire building (which I'm personally rather fond of)
*) Clever rondel mechanic
*) Over the course of a game, you can make lots of small, but interesting decisions
*) It takes careful planning to be the first to reach the required number of victory points without the other players knowing what hit 'em (otherwise you run in danger of facing several hostile players).
*) Rules are pretty clear and straightforward
*) Components are nice and of good quality (and they include extra zip bags!)

CONS
*) Not sure about that one, yet, but the start of the game might turn out to be pretty mechanic as players get more experience (a little like opening moves in chess, but with less options)
*) A single try with three players reveals that - with three players - the game might turn into an army buildup race, with no-one daring to openly attack, because they fear the 3rd player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game plays with a feeling like a race.  Players scramble to claim lands and get the resources built up in orer to fuel the next steps.  If you get bogged down in combat, you'll fall behind.  If you are efficient and maintain a good pace, you'll come out on top.  The game play is solid and can be quick if the players do not get locked into analysis paralysis.  Combat is not forgiving and if you are involved, your best chances of survival in the game are to bring others in (down?) with you.  It is important to watch for opportunities to claim personages when possible.  Don't underestimate the value of the personages claimed through advancements.  These are much simplier to get then generals or even some building goals.  Play balance is entirely dependent on player balance.  [GTI: TGSD]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fantastic game for my taste.

While I also like Mare Nostrum a lot too, this one is far easier to get people to play, and is less vulnerable to dragging on (which great balance can cause), as this game is as well balanced as Mare Nostrum.

Scanning some reviews from my 'analyze' and other respected reviewers, our one house rule seems to address the all the negatives I've read (that matter to my taste) at a fundamental level.  We shrink the board with low player counts.  We place grey or black player disks, not used by any player, on territories to make them out-of-play.  So the space is smaller.  This raises the interaction level, which underlies the main reviewer negatives against it.  So turtling, lack of vulnerable temple production, etc, all becomes not viable with high and early interaction. Also, the game is not really 'solvable' when interaction interrupts the 'solution'.

The flipside of this is 'be wary of 3 player games on a full board'.  The game totally breaks down in this case on several fronts, as other reviewers point out.  It is both transparent and drags on in this configuration.

More good news is that a 2 player variant works exceptionally well, as you'd expect from a relatively straight and abstract wargame...just constrain to a very small map.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
2 - 6 Spieler. Gutes Zivilisations-Spiel
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Have tried this once - a small 3 player game, but the rating could certainly go up from here. 

Very nice smooth civ game - this is basically what I want out of a "Civilization" board game... ease of play, nice components.

What's even better is that I can bring it to the table for Eurogamer friends and they won't be offended. It's really something that everyone can enjoy.

------------

After a second try with a few more people, it seemed to drag a little bit. I can't confidently say that this was a result of having 5 people, or if it was just that everyone was actually in the mood for BSG instead of a strategy game.

--------

The more I play this game, the more it becomes a staple. It's extremely simple so can be explained in as little as five minutes. Meanwhile, it easily fills multiple niches: civ building, combat, negotiation, saber rattling, at a VARIOUS number of players. With so little downtime, it's as good at 3 as it is at 6. 

After much much deliberation, I'm upgrading this to the hall of heroes: It's a 10.

Slightly unsatisfactory that turn order should determine the winner and that there is no compensation for this in the set up.  For example, extra coins for the later players.

The novel feature is the 'rondel', which works well.  The rest of the game I feel as if I have 'done' before.  The turn play is quick, even so our first game took over three hours.  Bookkeeping is simple as there is no loss of VPs gained, which is good.  The game appears to force the requirement to fight for temples near the end of the game, which is just about right. Happy to play again but there are more exciting games around.

Now played with four.  Much better than five, but other three players lost interest as I had a seemingly unassailable lead. Disappointing that the game can be won without a single battle taking place.  Maybe the cards could be scaled down with fewer players.  I want to like this game but it keeps throwing up issues.

RATING: 8
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Deutscher Spiele Preis 3rd Place 2006[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 12/22/06, Rated: 9[/i]

Ah, honeymoons.  They are wondrous, anxious, and exciting.  Such is my response after my first few plays of [i]Antike[/i].  But before I wax on about this game, let me tell you a little bit about myself.

I love turn-based, 4X (eXplore, eXpand, eXploit, and eXterminate) games for the PC.  I've logged weeks' worth of time with such titles as [i]Master of Orion[/i], [i]Master of Magic[/i], and of course the [i]Sid Meier's Civilization[/i] series.  The complexity of that genre doesn't translate well to a board game: a lot of the "bookkeeping" can get overwhelming if you don't have a computer crunching all of the numbers behind the scenes.  Trying to approximate a computer's behavior at the gaming table takes up a lot of time, and usually a ton of fiddly rules.

[i]Antike[/i] is the long-awaited exception that proves the rule.  It is so incredibly streamlined, that it captures the essence of a 4X PC game without inflicting horrendous calculations or player down-time.

The much-discussed [i]rondel[/i] is the engine at the heart of this game.  Simply put, it shows the eight available actions.  In a fit of elegance, each player can move up to three spaces clockwise around the rondel for free each turn.  She can choose to go further, but she must pay resources to do so.  This balance between resources and actions is exquisite.  The rondel develops a personality of its own.  You can envision the epochs of your civilization as they pass.  Certain periods are flush for resources (Iron and Gold, which are close to each other), while others are more militant (Maneuver and Arming).  Gauging your opponent's position within the rondel cycle is also important.  Did he just pass "Arming?"  If so, then he is ripe for an attack!

There is no random element to this game, which is highly attractive to me.  The outcome of a battle is perfectly predictable; there are no dice involved.  The simplicity of the mechanics permits players to focus on strategy and diplomacy.  This game can be highly social, and an astute player will avail herself of those options.  Each side is identical in its abilities, which is a slight drawback.  I would have liked to see advanced options that give each civilization its own unique "flavor," perhaps in the form of a starting technology, an extra resource, or a military unit.

I'm infatuated with [i]Antike[/i] at the moment.  Its weight scales well with players of varying levels of interest or skill.  I think it is a great way to introduce new players to civilization-building games, and therefore it is a welcome addition to my collection.

[url=http://www.boardgameswithscott.com/?p=20]BGWS Review[/url]

RATING: 9

RATING: 4
I've only played this game about 6 times.  The game has an interesting mechanic.  It uses a rondel to choose what you can accomplish on a turn.  The rondel is like a spinner without a spinning arrow.  Instead you move a marker up to three spaces ahead, and everyone has their own marker.  This game has no "luck" element.  Not a single thing is dictated by chance.  It reminds me of chess in that manner.  And if you choose to be aggressive and war like in the game, you are guaranteed to lose unless every other player is also doing the same.  This game really penalizes the aggressive player.  I like the luck factor in games, so I don't particularily enjoy this game.

RATING: 6
The first 50% of the game is peaceful expansion with a hope to grab early victory points by advancing your know how. The next 25% is running your economic engine game to crank up a war machine for the last 25%, which is about conquest. On the whole, I like the mechanics of the game, which can be summed up as follows. If you want to hurry up and use what you're getting right now, it will cost you a little extra to do so. But if you want to plod along more slowly, you can do so at lower cost, but you will miss out on good opportunities. That said, it's [i]very[/i] important, especially early on, to go for gold to get as many know how victory cards as possible. Failure to do so will leave you with a large thriving empire, but few victory points for innovation. I thought this was somewhat against the theme of the game. I also question the idea that land troops should be able to attack ships and vice versa. But those are necessary abstractions for playability. That said, I really like that the turns are very fast, and it's relatively easy to plan ahead for what you want to accomplish.

RATING: 3
[19/1/06] Upon reviewing the below and duly considering my feelings for this game, I must say I was way too generous in my earlier rating (5.5). After all, I'd choose Eggert Spiele's Global Powers any day over this - not that I like that design either...
----
This game has recently become quite a favourite of some of my gaming friends. Thus, I was eager to try it out and expectations were correspondingly high. I'm saddened to say those were disappointed. 

I feel there's too great a rich-get-richer problem. And the starting positions at the centre seem to be at a disadvantage compared to those located on the periphery. 

Also, the turn mode just doesn't seem exciting enough. And the attritional nature of combat often will only lead to a third party being the winner of any conflict.

I can't help thinking Mare Nostrum is a better game. Particularly with the new rules which adress the issues the rules had as published.

That said, its shortish playing time is a redeeming factor. Yet probably not enough to make me want to play this one.

Looking beyond the game mechanics, the game is for my taste also too generic in terms of its theme. The geography is uniformly bland - particularly due to the even and unhistorical distribution of resources. A little variety (wealthy and poor regions, uneven resource distribution) etc would have IMO been instrumental in balancing positions and making matters a whole lot more thematic and interesting in terms of gameplay. IMO the designer erred on the wrong side of abstraction when he could have easily avoided doing so without complicating the rules in any manner.

As things stand, the game is hardly evocative of antiquity and the game might have just as easily be about intergalactic civilizations...

RATING: 4
8 plays

I find this game . . . frustrating.  

It seems to offer multiple paths to victory, yet most games come down to a single military thrust to destroy a temple.  This is despite the fact that military campaigning is *not* a viable path to victory given how the system punishes the attacker.

Antike challenges players who begin in central board positions, bearing in mind that futility is challenging.  I have never seen the Greeks or Romans win.  I have rarely seen the Phoenicians loose.

Antike gives you a taste of empire building, but the favor is vanilla as your people have no attributes, skills, or even geographical advantages over others.

It uses an innovative and interesting mechanic in the Rondel, but the mechanic gets tiresome as does the feeling of never having the option to do what you actually want.  There's no logical reason that actions on the rondel can't be done in any order you choose except the game's rules - isn't that the definition of a "gamy" rule?

It's a pleasant way to spend a couple hours building, planning, nurturing; that usually ends suddenly in a single turn's ahistoric blitzkrieg.

It's a game I wanted to love but couldn't and each session lowered its rating.

Now I doubt we'll ever play again.

If you're looking for the perfect civilization building game, I'm truly sorry to say - it still hasn't been made.

RATING: 6
The rondel provides a similar feel to Imperial where getting the pulse of the game - and the quickening of that pulse - is the heart of things. But unlike Imperial which has a shareholding aspect that allows the incentives and alliances to continue to morph and flux through the endgame, Antike is very constrained by the players-as-powers. Also, the very coarse scoring makes for some serious blocking which, for some might be interesting, but I found excessively capricious.

RATING: 3
Ultimately the game seems to depend more on manipulation of the perception of your opponents than the actual game mechanics -- which is a good and fairly subtle thing.  I'd rate it higher if I were not fairly burnt out on it; we played a lot for a while.

Be not afraid of spending resources for beneficial actions.  It is usually worth it, especially in the early game.  For instance, what is the absolute minimum number of actions required to build three temples?  How about 6 temples?  Now for 5 cities? 10? 15?  What about the technologies?  Now which specific sequence of actions is optimal for earning VPs?  Is it single-target or mixed-model?  Is there more than one path?  The answers may surprise you.

Update: Too transparent and thus easily calculated to be sustainably interesting.

RATING: 7.5
The rules are simple, with an incredibly clear and well-written rulebook. The components and presentation are very attractive and high quality. But does it succeed as a playable (short) light civilization-building game?  Perhaps the civilization building is a little abstract and a bit limited, but there are lots of intersting decisions to make. You're racing to get VPs, really, which are a finite commodity. There is no luck, which is also nice in a game like this.I particularly like the action wheel mechanic. You can take some actions, but others will cost you money to advance that far. The turns are subsequently very short (as you're only doing one action) and it cycles around quickly. Downtime is at a minimum, and is painless.There is also wide open room for negotiation. Everyone can see who has which VPs. If someone starts to draw ahead, or expand too aggressively....the others will unite to keep him in check. This effect has pluses and minuses. This aspect reminded me most of Vinci. But different than Vinci, there are so many turns that it doesn't feel random who "happens" to go over the VP limit on their turn. Here, it seems like a good player can position him/herself for the win by having multiple options for that last VP. Flexibility and good planning will win....unless your diplomacy is a liability.  A very satisfying game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I love the Rondel, who doesn't love it though? If there's one common theme in the reviews that I've seen, it is that everyone who has been within 5 paces of this game has instantly fallen in love with that thing.

So what about the rest of the game? For the genre, not bad at all. Fans of this type of game will likely love it. I'm not a fan of this type of game in general though. Antike is a light "build up an economic engine and/or army and then compete with players at the end" kind of affair, and it reminds me a lot of Tempus, albeit in a slightly gamier disguise. In Antike, there are 3 resources that one collects, and there are a few ways to increase your production capacity in those resources. Said resources can later be spent on various victory point mechanisms, some of which make your army better (which allows you to conquer certain areas for VP's), some of which give you technology (which is instant VPs if you are first in), and some of which let you build temples (which create more resources and can also potentially give you VPs). As I said, the overall flow of the game seems to be that players build up their areas a bit in the early going, then they build up a few units, and by the late game, players need to compete directly to get the last piece of the puzzle.

I give plusses to this game based on the generally rock solid pace, which is a huge boon in this genre, I give it negatives because it really does feel a lot like a Tempus type of game, where I strongly feel that nearly the entire game is about building up stuff earlier, and then everything simply is decided by the last frame or two, and that being off in a corner somewhere is generally an advantage. This isn't a Wallenstein corner level advantage, but it is still nice, and certainly benefits a person in that position. Really, the reason why this is a shade below average, and why I may be slightly overrating Tempus yet, is the way the combats in these games work really bothers me. Anyone who stays out of combat until it is nearly impossible to be retaliated against is at a huge advantage, because the resources that it takes to create armies is just a huge time sink. This is particularly true here, because the combat is excruciatingly bloody, with perfect 1-1 trades down the line, and no way to mitigate that effect. Hence, I feel that winning this type of game is often more about who started their civ in a region that had the opponent who was content to sit back without making military incursions than it is about simply being the best logistical planner. I do not mind logistics and planning games if they are done right (and tend to favour them actually), and that is really what is at the heart of the Antike system, the problem here is that inefficiencies can occur basically based on the choices of one's opposition, which is fine if the entire table is interfering with each other with approximately equal vehemence, because then everyone is disadvantaged, but any area of the table avoiding that sort of waste, either via agreement or simple placement, should roll over the combat heavy segment portion of the table most of the time.

The bottom line is that this isn't bad for what it is. I don't generally care for combat games, and this is doing well in that I would not mind playing it again, because the overall pace is good and the rondel really is a neat device; but I don't love this game, and I don't think it escapes the general limitations that I perceive in its niche.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Antike was pointed out to me I think because it was repeatedly mentioned in any list where the infamous and hypothetical 'Civilization Light' game was discussed. At one point I made a note to give it a try during Spiel, and was lucky enough to find it during its 2006-edition. Although I had a few doubts about the viability at three players (which was the number of our test game), I bought a copy, and began to play with other people. After several games (all at different player numbers), the feeling that Antike was not what I had hoped it to be grew ever stronger. It plays fast due to its very business-like mechanics, but at times they are boring and mindless excercises. [i]Oh, is it my turn already?[/i] is not an uncommong exclamation at my table. However, far more problematic turned out to be the realisation that they also turn the game into a barren campaign of ruthless efficiency where the first one to blink loses. Attacks are non-existent in the 3- and 4-player games; and in the 5-player game the attacker becomes a sitting duck for everyone else in the vicinity. The author of the game published a document in which dozens of units are destroyed to sack just one temple—an impressive use of the simple movement and combat rules, granted... But at the same time both civs are completely bereft of cannon fodder and are likely to be overrun in the next few turns. Therefore Antike suffers greatly from the fact that the attacker will not profit from his actions, pushing the game into the territory of trench warfare with a quick and unstoppable jaunt to the finish. Various ideas were offered to remedy the situation. Antike should be played in a much more tactical and less empire-building sense, acquiring VPs whenever the possibility presents itself. But that means that whoever happens to be the most efficient at turning resources into VP wins as there is not a smidgen of luck or uncertainty to take into account. You can let a computer calculate the optimum moves to get your 'economy' up and running as fast as possible, depending on whether you'd like cities, knowledge, or expansion. The variety is completely due to the players interacting with each other, making this title very prone to multiplayer chaos. I dislike such designs, and Antike is no exception.

Another thing which slowly began to work on my nerves is the rondel, Mac Gerdts' token mechanic. He used in every big box game of his (Antike, Imperial, Hamburgum, and hidden away in a horrible map, Princes of Machu Picchu) but he never addressed what I perceive to be its core problem. The rondel works in such a way that the steps you take along its 'track' have a fixed difference. Since Antike is a game of efficiency you will not be wanting to spend a lot of coins moving your pawn further than the standard three paces. But the rondel has been constructed in such a fashion that the pace of your 'engine' is deliberately broken up into inefficient and irritating steps. To me the rondel attains all the characteristics of a stair with steps at an improper height: there is simply no way to walk smoothly across it, always you are forced to break the pace at some point. Of course, were one to introduce a sort-of adaptive rondel, which took care of the problem, then there would need to be other mechanisms in place to reign in a runaway leader. In Antike, those do not exist, save for when the leader is beset upon by all players equally. However, geographical restrictions forbid this already.

Antike began to irritate me more and more, and after nearly three years of not having been out of its box I sold off this example of what happens if you turn players into human computers.

RATING: 3
I like this *much* less than its successor Antike II (which is outclassed itself for two players by Antike Duellum). The main reason is that in Antike (I), arming military units for only one iron is waaaay too cheap for my taste. This makes fighting against other players much too attractive in my opinion. Even when an opponent has the know-how for defense +2, you still only need to spend 3 iron to conquer an undefended city - that are only as many resources as founding one! Conquering it comes with the added bonus of substantially harming one of your opponents. Let's not even talk about how overly attractive attacking your opponents is before they develop both defense know-hows (although it is somewhat harder to get to them with the requirement to spend one movement for attacking a city compared to Antike II).
The missing defense bonus with no more than 2 personages and the missing neutral temples are two further disadvantages compared to Antike II, which make conflict more attractive.
Another drawback of Antike (I) is that player order is not balanced (like Antike II does with the Bellona card).
There are even some further details which Antike II improved (particularly being able to choose which resource to produce in cities when founding them and galley symbols in sea regions).

All in all, the old version of the game is just way too conflict oriented for my taste. This would be fine in a 2 party game, but I strongly dislike it in multiparty games. See my 8th general thought about games in my profile.

Antike was only nice insofar as it lead to the much better Antike Duellum and Antike II, but now that they are there, my advice is to stay well clear of the old Antike. Unless you prefer a cutthroat all-out attack fest in multiparty games, of course.

RATING: 7
This is a really nice game. Very simple rules, fast gameplay, little downtime, plenty of meaningful strategic choices.

The only things that keep me from rating this higher are three things, that in my view are design flaws:

1) It seems to be much harder starting on a position in the middle of the board than on the edges (there's no balancing for that).

2) There is a strong chance of people ganging up on the leader... this can go as far as leader elimination (seen it happening). At the end, the player who didn't enter this conflict tends to win. 

3) Kind of an inverse runaway leader problem: Say you loose a region to an opponent's invasion. That will give you less resources to work with which in turn slows you down. At the same time, your opponent gains a region and thus momentum, being able to conquer even more. I found it hard to stop this spiral and have seen it happening multiple times.

It still is lots of fun to play -- I should just really like to see those problems addressed in future editions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Rondel does a great job of making a no-randomness wargame feel fun and engaging.  Very stressful to have to plan offensives two or three turns out.  The tech tree does an awesome job of creating a cold war tension.  (If you buy Wheels, there is no more truce line, Mr Germanic Tribe.)  It has to lose a point for being of unbounded time.  A game could last thirteen years in a balanced state since nothing drives the flow towards a conclusion.

And more importantly it loses a point for the "8-tech bonus point".  The rule can be balanced and fair and elegant and whatever, but it enables the game to end on a passive note.  A four hour wargame can't end with an unstoppable sit on your butt and buy a point.  A normal tech victory is fine; you attack their gold producing temple or buy the tech first.  If the game required one less point to win, and then dropped the 8-tech bonus it would be a constant rollercoaster ending on a peak.  Not on a sigh.

Update: 7-3.  I've played enough war and euro-war games now to know that this is complete suck.  You can't blockade a port or raze a city or steal supplies.  These sound like hihg-wargame terms, but really it is just "Why can you still get iron if I have troops standing outside your city?"  Attacking accomplishes nothing unless they have a temple.  Without one, it is just throwing iron chits at each other while the rest of the table runs forward.  This is not a war-euro, it is pure economy.  

If somebody takes the first several turns doing nothing but earning money and building temples, and I do nothing but build ships are rush over to attack him, and I _still_ can't accomplish anything, then there is only one way to play.  Attacking needs to do something to create any tension.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players take on one of several ancient civilizations, and expand, build and culture their way to victory.  Turns are super quick - basically advance a dobber on the "roundel" and take the action you land on (take resources, build temples, troops, get smarter, or go conquer stuff).  You earn "historic personages", a nice name for victory points, for achieving goals.  The first half of the game is fun as you grow your empire, then the head butting begins.  The game works very well, and it only takes about 30 seconds to take your turn.  However it can slow down and get a bit stuck late in the proceedings as those last few points are struggled over.  Looks very nice in action.  Rating dropping... getting a bit same-y.

Played it again for the first time in 5 years and really enjoyed it.  So, don't over play it, and it's fine.

RATING: 7

Note that there is not an iota of hidden information. The late-game war can bog down with negotiators and mini-maxers, so this will determine whether of not this game is a chore. 

Game comes with two maps and lots of wood in the box. Another great fresh game from Eggertspiele. One little extra twist away from being the "grail" everyone is looking for...

RATING: 10
Wow!  I've been looking for the Civ-Lite every since I started playing Civ, and while this doesn't reach that idealized state it nonetheless is a very solid title and one of the most played games in our local board game group.

It's strengths include:

Scales well between 3-6, and accomodates six very well, which is a difficult feat to accomplish in a game with so much going on.

Turns are short with very little downtime.  Even with six players your turn can come around sometimes so quickly that you aren't even prepared to take it.  This gives the game a wonderful pace.

The Rondel!  This device is one of the more unique game mechanisms to come out in the last several years.  I hope other games can utilize it's strengths to put out interesting new titles.

In the end, with a large game group, I've shown this game to about 18 people so far and it's always a hit.  No one complains when it's suggested for the table, and people ask for more of it.

RATING: 7

This might be a series of battles or a peaceful empire building game, depending on your group's predominant style, but the main reason why this doesn't get a 9 or 10 is the rather dry handling of scientific advances and historical elements. Game material is of high quality and quite abundant, the rules are clear and include tactical tips, but miss out on some key points which most players will grasp intuitively anyway.

RATING: 6
A bit hard to get a good grasp on this after one unusual 6-player game, where the Egyptians (me) and the Phoenicians utterly failed to contain the Greeks and let him march to victory with ease, very little war, and certainly no protracted endgame.

It reminded me of Tempus, but I liked it better with its more varied strategy and open-ended length.  Some compare it to Serenissima, which I consider to be a much better game despite its fixed game clock, with more interaction than Antike and a better sense of momentum shifts.

Like everyone else, I like the idea of the rondel, but I'm not sure I like how it's implemented here.  The turn actions are so short that they turn the game into a very choppy affair, and to quote Skyknight X, allow the game to almost move too quickly.  Lack of downtime is fine (although I admittedly am not mortally opposed to downtime), but this might be a cure that ultimately sickens the patient.  The game felt strangled and monotonous at times, as we went around and around the rondel doing very little.

Definitely want to play again, and see if smarter play yields better results.

RATING: 9
Good light civilization building game, zero luck, nice mechanisms and ideas, great looking map, quick game-play.  A very clean and neat system that scales well.  

The two player variants described in the rules are inferior, but an excellent one has been proposed by BGG users, see the post here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/352200

Overall: superb.  If there is a weakness, it might be that it is pure strategy and that there are no random events, as one might expect from a civ game.  Strategic play can be trumped by your board position (e.g. if you're sandwiched between two hostile and aggressive players) and diplomacy, so the best player won't necessarily win.  I personally don't mind this, because I love games with diplomacy, but I can appreciate that there are people who don't care for this (if they want the game to be all strategy, and get frustrated that they lose just because other players gang up on them.)  Antike might even be best as a two-player game, because that eliminates the diplomacy and bad board position which some people dislike about it: it's just two players head to head, and diplomacy and board position doesn't really come into it.

RATING: 4
A great Euro civilisation game with all the pieces (colonisation, economy, technology, military, diplomacy) distilled down. I was pleasantly surprised; shorter and cleaner than I feared; however I'm not sure about its replayability.

Duration: Length is moderate even with 5 players because of limited actions, but downtime can spike when players discuss what to do. Bookkeeping is minimal.
Design: Other players complained about starting player advantage and positioning. There are fiddly mechanics (taking a move to siege and calculating city defences) and rules ambiguities (running out of coins, moving multiple armies at once) but it plays pretty cleanly.
Strategy: There were a variety of approaches (economic, expansionist, technologist, military) and always an attractive alternative nearby. Wars are expensive and require very specific objectives.
Components: Nothing special.
Theme: Ancient civilisations cleanly and well done.

RATING: 7
An excellent game, perhaps worthy of the "Civ-Lite" moniker it received.  The rules are straightforward, there is a tech tree of sorts, and when you look at the way certain elements work you can see a touch of Civilization heritage there.

The rondel mechanism is a fantastic idea, not only interleaving player actions but also placing restrictions on how fast a player may return to the same action.  I can see this being played for some time to come.

UPDATE 14AUG08:  Now the initial sheen has worn off, I feel compelled to reduce the rating for this one - down to a 7.  I just don't think this one has stood the test of time as well as other games.  It's still a good game and one I like to play; however it just doesn't see enough table-time to justify keeping it rated 9.

RATING: 9
Rating is for my only game, 2-players using the (amazing!) half-map variant available here. The game follows the line of typical "antique battles in the meditteranean" genre, but the very well done winning conditions make sure that you have to balance development and confrontation. As such, my girlfriend enjoyed it very much even though she's ususally not very fond of wargames.

The only drawback (and the reason I do not rate it a 10) is that the final points seems to be won in a "rush" where the player forgets all about reasonable development and just dashes for it, leaving his civilization crippled... This might in fact prove an advantage for the first player in a turn, who has just the "little edge" to start this sprint before the others.

A note on the rondel: this is a neat choice-limiting device, but it's in no way the heart of the game.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I hate to do this, the rules are so clear and simple, the pieces are nice, the board is gorgeous, the rondel *is* a nice feature, but it just doesn't add up to fun for two players.

The board is *huge*, even with four nations, it doesn't fill up until late in the game. There are only three resources, which are easily amassed once a few temples go up, and the coins (free at every turn) make sure its very rare to be short of anything. 

So once you've got a marble city temple ensuring easy temple building, and an iron city temple (or three) ensuring rapid troop production. Then its just a matter of spreading out a little and then sacking a temple or two. 

I'll guess that it looks better with more players, earlier conflicts, probably more competition for cities based on their output, etc. But then it becomes an elimination game (yes, I know you can't sack a player's final city, but so what? They aren't going to make a comeback.), which isn't very appealing either. 

My last gripe is the number of VPs suggested for two players makes the game take *forever*, and pretty much eliminates any variety in the path to victory. You simply *have* to fulfill all the different reward conditions to get enough. 

Lowering the needed number of VPs would seem at first to fix that by shortening the game, but then there would be no reason for the players to interact at all, they could just quickly expand in their corners, build some temples, etc. What a snooze fest that would be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The cries of an actual civ-lite game are accurate IMHO. We played our first game (no experience from any of us) and completed the game in just over 2 hours, including rules explanation. Turns play very quick, so downtime is very limited. I love the mutual destruction (no dice) combat rules and the rondel is ingenious. It epitomizes the "too many options but can only choose one right now" trademark of a good Euro. Winner of first game used know-how strategy primarily, and only used combat once, as a final blow to destroy a temple for their last ancient personage card. UPDATE - played 4 times now, and rating stands

RATING: 7
An excellent resource management and area control game. The game mechanic of moving round the wheel is very interesting as it offers players the opportunity to perform whatever action they desire on their turn, but sometimes at a cost. This means players often have to spend two or three turns planning towards something they want to do.

In the games I've played I haven't got involved in much combat as it generally seems to benefit any players not involved. I would tend to only go for combat if it means an instant VP reward to make it worthwhile.
Still, there are many possible routes to victory and I'm sure a combat heavy strategy could win if played correctly.

All in all it's a very interesting game and is actually quite simple to teach and not too long to play, coming in under two hours.

RATING: 6
It is described as civ-lite.

The game is fast paced and can be played in 1.5 hours. It holds up to six players.

Combat is predictable and mutually destructive. You win if you have numerical superiority. Both sides trade losses and the last man standing wins. This means that it is advisable to hold off from engaging in combat until the end of the game. 

The games that I have played have ended too quickly and abruptly. I would suggest modifying the game to make it so that you need an extra ancient personage.

RATING: 6.5
Multiple paths to victory.  The wheel mechanic makes for very quick turns, and provides the players with good control over their own destiny.  Next to no down time when playing with 4 players!  Components are of excellent quality, and I really liked the starting position/Cities cards and player aids.

The technology grid is easy to play with, and does not confer an unfair advantage to the first person to develop it (although they do get the personality card, i.e. 1VP).

Game plays faster than MARE NOSTRUM, excludes exchanges, and does not suffer as badly from leader bashing.  I would prefer randomized combat, but I found the 1:1 exchanges worked surprisingly well.

Game is a tad pricey at 51 euros, but well worth the investment as it will see a lot of table time.  Recommended!

RATING: 7
Civilization 'light'. A highly abstract civilization game that can be reasonably played in 2.5-3 hours. The rondel game mechanic makes turns blazingly fast, with almost no downtime between turns--move around the rondel, and do that one specific action, maybe found a city, and possibly claim any victory points. There is a simple economics model, as well as simple combat, which is mutual annihilation. Near the end, it becomes a pseudo-war game, where some conflict is necessary to get the last remaining victory points, by launching a blitzkrieg (with roads knowledge) and destroying your neighbors' temples.

Update: played this twice with three players. We played both sides of the board, resulting in different strategies and game lengths (1.25 hrs vs 2+ hrs). While 'cutthroat' is fun, there is a tendency for two of the players to achieve a form of detente, while ganging up on the third. The usual marble strategy doesn't work as players are unhindered from spreading quickly across the board in a mad dash to grab as much territory as possible first, before settling down to working up knowledge. Once a single player has grabbed a large chunk of the board, it is difficult to beat him since the scale of the resource production will outweigh the benefits of knowledge initially.

Update (05/30/09): After playing a meaty wargame like Successors for the first time, this was a welcome relief. I also recently played Imperial, which is a re-implementation of the Rondel mechanism. The more I play this, the more I appreciate the genius of the Rondel mechanism, the clean and simple combat, resource generation, and just about everything. It is fast and furious. Is it a wargame? Yes and no. There's very little combat, except towards the end, but compared to those other titles I played, this simply sings. I'm upping it to a '9'.

Update (08/06/10): I'm glad they're coming out with a 4th edition, as the combat rules need to be clarified with more examples. This is probably the most difficult concept to grasp for newbies.

Update (10/17/10) 19 plays: I'm starting to get bored with this, as this is one of those wargames where being left alone will surely give someone the win. If any two other players start fighting with each other, the non-combatant will probably scoop up the rest of the victory points. Also, there is too much advantage for stronger players being set up next to newer/weaker players, meaning there is a right-player binding issue.

Update (10/10/11): After a year, I haven't played much of this, as I try to avoid it. It's great for introducing newbies to board gaming, but is not providing the excitement I once felt about the game. Downing to an '8' from a '9'.

Update (02/10/13): Another year of avoiding this deterministic game. Considering that Clash of Cultures is now the go-to game for scratching the civilization game itch, Antike is collecting dust. Downing to a '7'.

Each player is managing an ancient civilization on the shores of or near the Mediterranean Sea. RATING: 8
Great elegant civ/wargame/eurogame that has no luck and multiple paths, probably the best civ-lite type game I have played to date other than La Citta which was quite good.

The Rondel is a very neat mechanic that allows you to easily plan several turns ahead while adding turn angst as you usually can't get to where you want to be. Paying to move further along the Rondel was a great idea that at least gives you the option while also adding some angst.

The turns in the first hour are unbelievably fast and even later it really doesn't bog down too much. My first game threw me off quite a bit as it is not a wargame in the traditional sense even though it looks like one, more of a euro wargame which requires an adjustment for any Grognards. I missed the point playing from old habits and was immediately worried about attacks and attacking rather than focusing on the many other goals that give you the points to win.  

I Like this more than Tempus as that game only has scoring at the end making some of your efforts seem somewhat wasted throughout, also the war in Tempus often isn't even a good option, here its a little more viable at the right times and it works better too. 

It did seem a tad long with 5 and maybe Morgan D is right as the last VP seems quite long to acquire so that would be easily adjustable. It could have a problem staying fresh but only if you over play it which shouldn't happen with all the great games to choose from these days. 

Other than that quite hard to find any faults in this game so far, no luck (You know exactly where you go wrong and why the winner won), double sided board for added replay, multiple paths, fast pace, great game.

Rating could rise with more plays

RATING: 8
I think this is the best Civ Lite game so far. I've yet to have a 3 or 4 player game take more than two and a half hours and have frequently come in under two hours, so it has the "Lite" side down. You have to accept the premise of a lite game, which is that everything is vastly streamlined:  there are only eight techs (arranged in four branches), there is only one city improvement (temples), there are only three resources (iron, marble, gold), there are only two military units (legions and galleys). That said, Antike captures the essence of the genre.  I'm happy to sacrifice trade in the interests of speed of play.  The multiple types of victory points are handled very elegantly and really make the game competitive and true to its theme of building a great civilization.  The endgame sometimes becomes a military struggle, which I think is a wonderful climax to a fun 90 minutes of gaming.  I love it.  Sometimes we use a house rule in board set up:  we try to distribute starting positions in a triangle (for 3 player games) or at each corner (for 4 player games).

RATING: 6
I have now played Antike twice, once with 4 players and once with 6, both times on the Western Med board. 

To sum-up: It's a clever, fast civ-building game and it's the closest we've got to a "playable" Civilization yet. 

But despite being closest, it's still not a proper replacement for that old classic: All the particulars which made Civilization the classic it is are still missing in Antike: No population expansion (just the usual build-armies-and-fleets instead), no trading, no sense of rise and fall of civilizations, no calamities, and a much weaker tech advance system. City-building is also much more abstractly handled in Antike - here a city is basically just an area control marker, rather than in Civilization's unique approach where cities interact with population in a number of interesting ways. 

Instead of "Civilization Light" we get a fast-moving game where each nation continuously expands (in territory, advances, temples, etc.) until one reaches the winning VP threshold. The action selection mechanism, using the roundel, is surprisingly clever and leads to many agonizing choices. Hit-the-leader problems are to a large degree eliminated, since VP are scored throughout the game, and can't be lost once gained. 

About the balance of the VP level threshold, in my two games it worked fine. Especially in the 6-player game where it felt like the game ended at just the right time. The 4-player game looked like it had the potential for a drawn out endgame (with people pursuing temple sacking just for the sake of that last VP), but luckily (?) we escaped that. However, I can easily imagine that playing to 12 VP with 3 players would lead to a long, horrible endgame. Luckily, the fix for this is easy; play to a lower amount of VP instead. 

Generally, my feeling is that the game works better with many players: With few players the board feels too open, and there is too little competition for the VP cards. Why these elements weren't scaled down with fewer players, I don't know: It does seem like an obvious approach (eg. play on only half the board and with a subset of VP cards with three players), instead of changing the VP threshold. 

All in all, I like the game. It's not - for me at least - the classic that Civilization is, but at least Antike is fun (for now, ask me again in a year), and is fast enough that it actually gets played.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This game is Diplomacy for ADD. More depth and choice of actions, and definitely need more player interaction that what we ran into.  No luck and tight scoring.

RATING: 4
2011-07-29

Initial Rating 8.0 (July 2010)

I thought Antike was better than it really is, but I still like it a lot. A strong point (for me) is that Antike has no luck whatsoever but sadly (for me) it isn’t a combat nor a civilization game, it´s more of a VP race game themed as an empire building game.You race to get VP founding cities, building temples, acquiring knowledge, controlling area on land and sea or destroying enemy temples. Another fun innovation (for me) was the rondel mechanic; it keeps the game flowing and minimizes a little the analysis paralysis that some people can experience.  Also the game is easy to learn and teach as the rules are very easy and intuitive.

On the bad side, the game is very unbalanced as the starting positions at the center have a steep disadvantage compared to those located on the periphery.

RATING: 7
Based on a solo play and a 5 player FtF. Thoroughly enjoyed both - won them both too, although the solo one was a lot easier!

It plays quickly and smoothly, you need to be logistically efficient with your moves to avoid falling behind if experienced players are in play, and it looks good. There are a number of different ways of garnering the required victory points (although you'll need to use most of them to win) but the strategy is in deciding in which order to go for the objectives. You also need to be flexible enough to deal with what the other players are doing. 

There are plenty of opportunities for negotiation/influencing, and it comes to a sudden end when one player meets the victory point requirement. While some commentators have criticised the sudden end, to me it steps up the level of tension when you know certain players are getting close to victory - you had better be getting close too!

RATING: 6
Much too long for what it is.  There seems to be only one or two viable strategies... just minimise the number of turns required to do what you want and you should do well.

With a five or six players, if the game is close, there aren't enough victory points for anyone to actually get enough points necessary to win!  Then, the first person to sack a temple wins in a sudden death situation.  Very contrived...

The end game play among competent players will then always be the same... defend  your temples until someone makes a mistake!  Not what I like to see in any game.  I doubt that I'll play it again with more than 4 players.

Trim the victory points by one across the board and you'll have much more exciting finishes with all numbers of players.  A variant giving "special powers" to each of the individual nations would also be a benefit, so some of the central powers actually have a chance to win.

My rating has gone up slightly only because I finally played a game with only 4 players and it clocked in at just over 2 hours... much better.

RATING: 7
The first time through, I thought it was one of the best games ever.  While it is really fun for 5-10 games, its appeal tapers off quickly.  

It's purely deterministic, which is ascetically pleasing at first.  But to go from understanding basic strategy to being really good at the game you have to master micromanaging. In chess, these details all fit under some metaphor of "attacking", "controlling the center," but in Antike, they are impersonal arithmetic.

If you just want to have fun with a game for a little while, this is a great buy.

RATING: 4
The action (such as it is) takes quite awhile to get going, as the first half of the game involves spreading out to acquire all the territories on the map. As "combat" is a deterministic one-for-one mutual elimination, the very threat of a nearby enemy arming himself interrupts your plans for getting production/temples/know-hows, forcing you to reply in kind by creating units. 

Players seem to score more or less at the same rate, and anyone who can get an edge in the amount of resources being produced will over time be able to dominate militarily and probably win a slight victory. I used the phrase "zero sum game" to describe the feeling the game left me with, and unfortunately it's much too long for what it offers--no surprise, no drama, predictable outcomes.

RATING: 5
Players each take on the role of an ancient civilization in its infancy - before the invention of the wheel even, if that is to be believed, but the game is in no way intended to be historically accurate beyond the general start locations on the board. The board is two-sided, so you can choose the lands of the Roman Empire in Western Europe or the lands of Alexander the Great in SE Europe and Arabia. On a turn, each player performs one action, founds cities if they are able, and then takes any personage card for which they qualify. Personage cards are gained when certain achievements are accomplished, such as building 3 temples, expanding to 5 cities, and being the first to develop a "know-how" (technology). The first player to collect a certain number of cards (depending on number of players) wins.

The actions are of three kinds: collect resources from cities (gold, marble, or iron), spend collected resources (to gain know-hows, build temples, or arm soldiers and galleys), or manouvre (move military and fight). With these few rules civilizations grow, develop and eventually go to war in pursuit of the prized personage cards.

Antike isn't without its problems, and most of those are in the end game, I think. The final accomplishment for most civilizations will be to destroy a neighbor's temple. This makes the focus at the end more on preventing your opponents from winning rather than pushing forward for the win yourself. If all civilizations are well-managed and fortified, I could see this end-game dance playing out for quite a while. Also, even though the game certainly makes no attempt to portray real history, it makes no sense that a civilization should win by making a last-ditch gambit to send their entire army to one location to destroy a city. It's as if time stops at that point and the civ has absolutely not a care in the world about what dangers it exposes them to the next day. Ultimately it's a bit of a letdown following what is otherwise an excellent game.

UPDATE: Every time I play this game, I'm slightly more disappointed in it than the previous play. I think I'm calling it quits now. This started as an 8 and now has settled at a final score of 5, where I think it will stay.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Picked up at Essen 2005, played one five-player game. Preliminary rating would be 8 or possibly 9 as I liked it a lot.

Of the five people playing, the two gamers (one of them being me) liked it a lot, as did the wife (who is somewhere inbetween a non-gamer and gamer). The two non-gamers (they like Settlers, TtR and lighter games) weren't that keen on it but still thought it was OK (or so they said).

What was most surprising to me when playing was the lack of conflict, I had expected it to take a much bigger part than it did (in our game we only had about five battles, if that). In fact, one of the non-gamers (or should that be Settlers-fans) commented that it felt a bit like multi-player solitaire. I suppose there is some truth to this, as you at least in the beginning of the game seem to concentrate on your own civ without too much contact with the others.

One thing I liked about it was the pace, turns come around very quickly and there is very little down time. Strangely enough the Settlers-fans commented that it was a very long game even though it lasted shorter than a typical Settlers-game, whereas the three of us that liked it didn't think this at all. The 'Rondel' is a very nice mechanism, and as I found out you can get a bit 'out of sync' on it when the other gamer moved some troops too close to me for comfort, so even though he never attacked me I had to temporarily abandon my strategy to instead waste a few turns to get iron and build units, which left me in the wrong place on the rondel.

Hmm, this turned out a bit long. Maybe it should have been a session report.  ;)

Looking forward to play again, €38 well spent.

RATING: 7.3
Yes, this IS civ-lite.  It has war, economy, technology, historical nations etc etc.  So why has it not been crowned as such?  Perhaps its because its deterministic - there is no luck at all - or perhaps because its a bit generic (no special civ powers) - but its certainly got the important bases covered (except for trading).  My biggest gripe is a common one with civ games - the scope of the game indicates that one turn equals many years (50-100), yet armies can only move one region per turn.  Makes no sense at all, but its common with almost all of these games.  Also, the player who avoids combat most will generally win ... I guess its like Pirate's Cove in that respect, he he.  I guess I agree with Chris Farrell that in the end the diplomacy aspect will determine the winner more than anything else ...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.2
Played this one with 6. It has a nice board and components (like ship meeples and soldier meeples). The game design demonstrated a great marriage between theme and mechanism, like the know-how abilities (sail increasing boat movement, market increasing resources, etc), and the application of the different resources (marble for temples etc). 

The rondel was a pretty cool mechanic. But somehow, since the rest of the game had such tightly-integrated theme & mechanics, the abstractness of the rondel stuck out to me. But I did like how it was built such that rondel slices that were complementary to each other were always just out of reach. This required some interesting choices and careful planning.

I liked the diversity of achievements required to get victory points - it encourages strategic complexity and multiple routes to victory. Like Galactic Emperor, this one is a pretty unforgiving game, where if you make a few bad decisions early on, they will haunt you for the rest of the game, and it seems nigh impossible to mount a come-back; so, there seems more of a strategic learning curve to this game than many other games. 

With 6 players, the Greeks (center position) were attacked on 5 fronts. All other armies were attacked max by 3 fronts. So there is a balance issue here; if not a persistent disadvantage, there is at the very least a steeper learning curve for effective strategy in the center. I like games with asymmetry, but I like the asymmetry to have advantages and disadvantages for each side (like in Lord of the Rings: The Confrontation (Deluxe Edition)). In this case I don't see any advantages that make up for the disadvantages of being surrounded on all sides and vulnerable to attack by more armies than anyone else.

Also, since the ability to do pretty much everything you would want to do in the game is tied to resources, and resources are tied to the cities you own, getting attacked and losing cities can lead to a pronounced negative feedback loop, and a "runaway loser," poor-get-poorer effect. I particularly don't like this dynamic, and I believe this design is unique to this game relative to other (modern) games I've played.

So, given the above issues, and given the fame and hype I'd heard about the game, this was overall a disappointment for me. But, I would try again maybe with 4 players, so at least the balance issues wouldn't be present, and see how that plays out. I'll give it a tentative 6 out of 10 pending further play. But, if I want to play a game that involves conquest, nation building, resource management and area control, I would pick Wallenstein over Antike any day.

RATING: 7

RATING: 8
Antike is one of those games that beg for several plays before a definitive judgement as the most intuitive starting strategies are the ones that lead to a very dry ending. But, for those who don't know much about the game, I'll try to resume the basic principles.

The most important of all the mechanisms that the game employs is the rondel: this simple, yet deep, way of controlling the actions that reduces downtime to a minimum while forcing you to have a defined strategy. The other key mechanism is the way that you score points. Basically, you can score by expanding, investigating and conquering, though the points you can acquire each way are limited, so you must keep scoring constantly or you might find that people have drained the points you where aiming for. Lastly, the combat: you remove units from both sides at a 1:1 ratio (maybe more if you are trying to conquer a city), which makes it deadly for both sides and forces so it forces you to think precisely where and when to strike.

So, what I mentioned before about the several plays: on the first game almost everybody tries a growth strategy from the beginning to an end, which leads to a drain of all the non-bellicose points and several rounds of stagnant and growing armies, while everybody tries to wait like vultures for the first conflict to arise and then jump on the weakened sides. Once you play more games this tendency disappears and, usually, at least two or even three players are aiming for military points once they've done a minimal growth. This makes plays much more interesting and different one from another.

The game's biggest black spot are the first turns, as there are only three viable opening moves. So, once you decide if you are focusing on technologies, temples or cities, you are on fully automated mode and not making any more decisions until the fifth round or so. An even worse repercussion of this is that these starting moves need to be explained, as if a player fails to see them, he'll be severely hampered.

Would you like Antike? Well, if you want a game where luck reduces to starting positions, based on growth strategies but where caring for military is mandatory and has a light yet interesting theme, I would give it a try. And if you feel that the game ending is a bit stagnant, play it a bit more, as that will most likely reduce that feeling.

Rating: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Optimal number of players:
:thumbsup:5
:thumbsdown:2~3

The wheel for choosing an action is an interesting mechanism. Concentrate on the different cards (VP) and how to get them. Although combat may be needed towards the end of the game or earlier depending on your strategy.

RATING: 6
I was expecting Antike to try and be a quick Civ lite. Guess the ever-elusive "2 hour Civ" is still waiting for its inventor. Which doesn't mean Antike is a bad game. It's an area control and rescource management Euro with a war and civ theme. Not too deeply implemented though, but it's quite neat and original in some ways.

Timing your actions seems a big part of the game, as is keeping an eye on your opponents' position on the action rondel vs. their resources, to anticipate their next move. Especially in the endgame with the all out temple destruction, where you just need to protect a couple key sites and abandon everything else. All that building and expanding until the late mid-game doesn't pay off in points as good as the final conflict, so can this be played effectively in any other way? All the criticism about Antike being too samey from play to play could be right on target, but it's compelling enough that I want to test it some more.

Only played with 4, I suspect this would be a lot better with 6, or 5 as well, because the map gets tighter.

RATING: 6
The Rondel (the way you choose your action each turn) is a beautiful mechanism. The game's combat is moderate, and quitre comparable in feel to Wallenstein, but with absolutely no luck; actually there's no luck at all in this game. 

The fact that this game has more wood than any other that I own, coupled with the fact that it has 2 maps right out of the box, makes this one a winner; and I have to believe that my rating will rise with more players. This game has tremendous tension, and that will certainly be bolstered with more players involved.

If you can get you hands on this one, it's well worth it.

Update: With the advent of other Rondel based games this one drops a point.

Update 2: More plays in recent times. I don't know if it's because we've played so many more games now, or if the experience at this particular game has contributed to better play, but this one returns to an 8. 

Update 3:

This game can suffer from new players versus experienced players. The advancements have to be equally gained first in order for equal play else there can be a runaway leader. That being said, I think the game is certainly all about the advancements and lacks the diversity in paths to victory that later rondel games have.

RATING: 5
[Played once]
The "eurogame version" of Civilization. Streamlined (e.g. tech tree is reduced to only 8 progresses), diceless, abstract (personalities like kings, scientists, etc. are just names given to some conditions bringing Victory Points), simple (only a few pages of rules), with acceptable lenght and a clever idea (the "wheel of actions").
It's still quite confrontational and involving a lot of diplomacy (i.e. you'll have to manipulate the players inducing them to harm someone else).
Probably better with more players.

RATING: 7.5
Very good game except for:
1. Turtle.
2. Battle is expensive for the attacker. Unless its for the (final?) rush of VPs, un-calculated battles serves no purpose except to lose the game.
3. Not enuff meeple army. Center player without coastal cities are disadvantaged as they can only build land-units (17). They thus have to adopt a different (more difficult?) strategy.

Update: Dropped 8 » 7.5. Still like this game. Its just that I found ways to play this game which can lead to it been a really boring affair.

RATING: 7
I can see that some people are disappointed that this isn't necessarily the "war" game they expected.  It's really a race to build the most successful civilization.  There are 5 ways to gain a victory point: for every 5 cities built, for every 3 temples built, being the first to invest in a new discovery (know-how), for every 7 water areas controlled (by ships) & for every temple destroyed.  

The rondel is what really sets this game apart.  It makes for tough decisions as you can only choose/perform one type of action on each turn & you can only move 3 spaces along the rondel for free.  The one-action per turn keeps the game moving at a steady clip.

Combat is diceless & simple, basically you need one more unit than your opponents defense to conqueur.  

This could be a war/diplomacy game depending on the game group personality (just attack more) but I see it more as a resource management, area control game.  I'm looking forward to see how future games turn out. 11/2005

UPDATE: Knocking down a point, some from less interest, some from the fact that [gameid=166857] is a slightly improved version. 01/2015

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Played once with three players.  An empire-building game wherein players acquire resources to build units to enable them to explore (and/or conquer) new areas so they can build cities and acquire more resources.  Victory, however, is determined by meeting specific goals, most of which are not based on expansion or military prowess.

The game is very clean: there are no special power cards, no rule-breaking abilities, only a few upgrades, and battles are entirely deterministic.  This allows for a very strategic and tactical game where every player is on the exact same footing, and luck has little to do with the outcome.

The turn mechanics are also very cool: all the actions that can be carried out on a turn are distributed around a circle; players can only choose one action per turn, and their options are limited by what's close to them on the wheel (so, for instance, there's only one point on the wheel where you can build troops; if you're not close to that point, you won't be able to build troops).  This forces players to plan their moves in advance, and also provides for interesting limitations to the game (e.g., you can't move your units on every turn, and if you do choose to move your units you can't get any resources on that turn).

Victory points are earned by achieving pre-set goals (e.g., building X number of cities, exploring a certain amount of sea-spaces), many of which have nothing to do with attacking other players.  There are a limited number of points available for each type of goal, though, so players are encouraged to take different strategies.  And, eventually, the game is likely to head to conflict (as one goal is to destroy other players' property).  

These factors all combine to make a great, brain-burning game.  King-making is a problem (as the leader will logically be attacked by everyone at the end of the game), and there is the possibility of hurt feelings (as attacking is an option, and attacks can be very damaging), but if you know that going in you'll be fine.

One problem that can occur is that it is nearly impossible to go into the "way-back machine", due to the number of units on the board and the complexity of how they can move.  Thus, it might be a good idea to make it clear that normal house rules about allowing players to take turns back are revoked for this game.

RATING: N/A
Ancient nations battle for supremacy in this economic and warfare game. Players choose to earn income in marble, gold, or iron, build temples, move armies and fleets, build armies and fleets,  advance in progress and build cities. Players earn victory points by building cities, being the first to advance in progress, have 6 temples, etc. The first phase of the game is an economic type game, but it quickly changes to one of military warfare. Like any wargames, perception, and being outnumbered by your opponents will play a role. It's a simple game to play and the rules are easy to understand.

This is not to say the game isn't bad, because it probably is the best light civ game out there, but I've finally come to the conclusion that if you are going to play a civ game, you bite the bullet and play for at least 3-4 hours.  If you refuse to play over that period of time and still want a civ-builder, then Antike is the game for you.

RATING: 2
This weekend, after two years on a friend's shelf, we played Antike again.  And it will be my last.

Here's what happens if you start a war you can't win in one turn, or if one of your opponents does the same to you: You lose the game.  Both you and your opponent are virtually assured of loss, because the sludge of war insists you invest your resources in these efforts.  Meanwhile, the other players run away with the other victory points.

After 90 minutes of watching myself lose because another player thought I'd back down, I'm done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very simple rules that make for an elegant and higly entertaining game, since the very few turns (at least in a 6 players game). Really streamlined in spite of being old (almost 10 years!). Its very few rules have a great reach.

The gameflow is very fast, and the game lenght can be even less than advertised.

The rondel really works well in this game.

Things I did not like (the missing 3 points for the 10):
-> Attacking has a high cost; it leaves you weak and vulnerable to counterattacks. This promotes Cold War situation and a final blitzkrieg attack to get the last points you need to win.
-> Because of that, it's strategically easier to attack someone who has just attacked or been attacked (the typical gangbang that can be seen in so many games)
-> The map is uneven (Mediterranean) for the greek (the rest are OK); he shares frontiers with 5 players (the rest only share with 2 players). With 4 players, Greece is in the middle too).
-> [Very minor issue] It's not easy to see what the cities produce once the discs are in place (it can be still be seen without moving them).

[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/104955/antike-duellum]Antike Duellum[/url] is the 2 player version of this game, and it includes a few changes I like a lot. I include them here because they can be house ruled for this game too:
-> When you found a new city, you decide the resource it provides (pay 3 different resorces, as always, plus 1 coin for every neighbouring city producing this same resource).
-> 6 different technologies (6 are too few for many players, but I'm not convinced level 2 of the land and sea movement are good for the game; I think it promotes blitzkrieg attacks to get the final points you need to win - to be confirmed with more gameplay)
-> Don't need an additional movement to conquer a city (entering a region is enough)
-> Movement of another player cannot be allowed if you have troops in that region (obvious in a 2 player game; I don't know if that would be good or bad in Antike; it does not happen often).

One idea to be tested: do not give coins each round, give them as reward for attacks/conquering cities.

RATING: 9
I really like the Rondel, which is indicative of how well thought-out this game is. Plays smoothly and quickly, is easy to grasp, and intriguing. 

If I had one preference it would have been that the starting civilizations have varying strengths, rather than all the same attributes, but it appears the designer decided to concentrate on the 'game' aspect of this effort, and I respect that choice. So, that's a minor criticism, as is my wish that the battle system allowed for some random element, so that an underdog could win at times. 

"Antike" does have many potential strategies, plus a double-sided board of the Eastern and Western world of ancient history. For what the designer intended it to be, it's virtually a perfect rendition, as rules are minimal, but it absolutely elegant to play, as even though there is much to consider, moves can be made quickly, especially using the Rondel. 

I love all the great wooden bits, too: The game is absolutely beautiful to behold once set up and played; I've even take photos of my final board positions at the end of a contest. 

The rule book is also one of the best written and illustrated I have seen. As regards best number of players, more is better, as the game and its strategic options really shine with a more crowded board: with 3, for instance, I found it too easy to move around and claim large chunks of territory before competing civilizations had to vie over territory. Purchased used, October 2008.
