RATING: 5
As a dudes-on-a-map game, I'm already not favorably inclined toward Antike, but I think this game has some very serious flaws.  This is a victory point game that's based on fulfilling certain achievements, and while multiple people can get each one, they do run out before everyone can get there.  So if you're a little behind the curve, you get left out of points that would be easy to achieve.  Some starting positions are better than others because they provide more access to water, and one of the achievements is for building a large fleet (there is no corresponding achievement for armies, and the two are otherwise equivalent).  Conquest-based achievements are particularly hard to fulfill, as an opponent has to build a temple that's accessible to your forces, and the movement/conquest rules force you to telescope your intentions, giving the defender lots of time to react (also, there are large defensive bonuses and no offensive bonuses).  By the end of the game, it's very difficult for even the leader to score the last achievement or two, while all the other players are forced to stop him, being practically out of the running themselves.  Not a good experience.

(1 play)

RATING: 5
First play with 5 and the board becomes "busy" as the session progresses and I agreed with one player's comment that he couldn't be bothered working stuff out when it came to decision time. (So 4 players maybe better). I like the experience that you can "peacefully" develop your empires, along various avenues to collect the victory points, such as occupying 7 seas, 5 cities, build 3 temples etc. More importantly, once you claim a vp card it can never be taken from you, so the game will end. But the game seems to be programmed that conflict will be inevitable, and this is where things become confusing: combat is diceless as in Vinci, and there are empire value modifiers (via development)for defence and overcoming marching fatigue. I was apathetic to this, so pursed a peaceful path, but some players may want some action. Combat is dry, and can result in the Steve Bradbury effect, in weaking combatants will allow a third to pull through for the win. The rondel makes for quick and interesting angst, and I like the freebie "wild" coin and the flavour of the vp cards. But since taking a stronger interest in history, I too would like to see a civ game capturing the unique empire qualities/geography etc.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
-

An engaging conquest game with some similarities to Vinci and Empires of the Ancient World. Those temples! They can make AND break you. The cliché, "location, location, location" couldn't have been more appropriate than in relation to these temples.  The dichotomy of 'temples' is supreme in this game. You need to build temples in order to win the game, but owning temples make you a target of attacks, since your temples are victory points to your opponents!

One potentially major problem I can envision with Antike is the way it can slow down towards the end when players are struggling for their last one or two victory points. I think the game can drag on for long stretches of time with minimal progress or change.

This is a tentative rating after two plays. A couple more plays are needed to digest the strategies of and potential issues with the game. But I still enjoyed both my games (the first time at BGG.CON, Nov. 3-6, 2005.)
Update 03/05/06. Increased my rating from 7.5 to 8.

RATING: 5
Normally I would have given this a higher rating but had to drop it based on a recent play.

First, the turn order problem is a HUGE weakness and design flaw. First player wins a disproportionate amount of times according to other geeks. This makes sense if everyone is grabbing victory points at the same pace.

Second, this was the author's first rondell game and I think he has improved with his most recent designs, namely Navigador is a superior game in my opinion and I would prefer to play that.

Third, the combat is messy for city captures (another design weakness). And I personally don't care for the one for one exchange during combat. I prefer a variant where we rolled some dice in addition to troops, which I think made the game better.

RATING: 7
Was a bit disappointed. 

The rondel is nice mechanism to limit which actions are readily available.
 
Just found the game play with the deterministic combat to be dry. Looks to be scripted - the only successful strategy I've seen is marble early (get production going), gold middle (grab some tech points), Iron late (get the final point via expansion). Almost any other way puts you either behind the production curve or the tech points curve. 

Deciding where and when to attack is a pure math affair. Getting attacked doesn't look as bad as it first appears (once you get a card you can't lose it), and the bleeding for 2 sides sets up a third to come in and stomp both. Being one of the center powers like in Civ is tough. 

However that is a result of a purely deterministic system. So if you like to lay your plans and never see them go awry this is for you, if you like to take chances - pass of this one.

While it plays fast there are better civilization games (Conquest of Paradise, Vinci, old Civ itself).  

BTW I Played Imperial first and found it to be a much superior game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Played about half a dozen times now, and I love it.  

Antike is a great game.  The fact that it takes 5 minutes to explain and two hours to play is a plus point.

With this section of the market already overcrowded with entries like Mare Nostrum and Serenissima, I would rate Antike far higher than the competition.

What I particularly liked was not just that there were multiple paths to the victory points, and multiple paths to the resources that would take you up those paths, but the way that the game neither encouraged nor discouraged conflict - and the effect on the players involved was not catastrophic either. 

I'd also say that it was medium weight, as it was a very simple system; perhaps unique amongst this sort of game, the turns moved very quickly indeed, sometimes taking just a few moments for each player in the rounds where people were developing their positions.  

The three-player game works FINE when played to 10VPs, the six-player game is also great (but a bit short), and a military strategy is viable WHATEVER the number of players.  Top game.

RATING: 3
Rating based on playing with 6 players, which I've done twice: I will NEVER play it again with 6 players. If I ever play it with less, I will adjust the rating to average it out based on my new experience. Eliminates the luck factor of Risk yet retains the same flavor of player interaction.  Problem with a 6-player game is that two players get royally ripped and four get nice safe corners from which to build, because UNLIKE Risk, there are no 'choke points' for protection in the middle of the board, leaving two people to play the lambs circled by a pack of wolves.  Substantial changes to the map would tend to even this out greatly.  (Hint: not EVERY war game has to be based on the countries surrounding the Mediterranean Sea.) Ignoring that, the game has a wonderful mechanism to make growing and defending an empire challengingly complex and varied.  I expect to play this one with 4 players or less some time. Totally aside from number of players, it can be a pain, and drag on a long time if everyone analyzes every aspect of their move before actually moving, which is (unfortunately, in my opinion) possible in this game. Due to the luck factor being eliminated, the endgame becomes an exercise in solving a perfectly solvable mathematical puzzle -- more than a war game -- and thus detracts a great deal from my potential for enjoyment (despite my appreciation for some mathematical puzzles).

RATING: 8
I would say that Antike does a great job of being a civ light game.  It was much lighter than I was expecting it to be, but I can see how it could get heavier with more players. (I played with three.)  The only phase that takes any time is the manouver phase, and it would be longer with more players because there is more to consider.  I really like that they included a tech tree (perhaps it should be called a tech bush because it is so short). Along those lines, Gerdts incorporated many different civ elements into a game with a fairly quick play time (obviously longer than Roll Through the Ages, but it seemed sorter than La Citta one of the only other civ-light style games I have played). I like Antike a lot and would recommend it to anyone looking for a civ style game that can be played in an hour and a half.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2
Antike is a challenging strategy game about evolution and competition among ancient civilizations. Ancient nations create cities, build temples, sail the seas, and discover new principles of science and technology. Their legions and galleys open new settlements and defend their people against attacks from their enemies. Two scenarios can be chosen as the game board is two-sided. Players choose from Greeks, Romans, and Germanic tribes and Phoenicians, Carthaginians, Persians, Arabs, Egyptians, and Babylonians.

Every nation tries to win ancient kings, scholars, generals, citizens, and navigators for themselves. The nation that acquires a specified number (depending on the number of players) ancient personalities first will win the game!

Lead one of these nations to victory! But watch out for your enemies, as they will want to conquer your cities to destroy your temples. The game depends not on the luck of dice or cards, but on thoughtful plans and skillful diplomacy.

Only downside really is that it can seem to drag on a little towards the end and people tend to gang up on someone they don't like (me usually).  Highly recommended for those wanting an elegant, simple, yet deep, civ, economic, war game with no luck.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Likes
I love civilization games and also the Rondell action selection mechanic that this game employs. There are some agonizing decisions to be made each turn as you choose which action to take and try to get your timing right with respect to what other players are doing.

Dislikes
I hate that Antike is a basically a race for VP. I like my civ games a bit more ponderous and Antike plays too frenetically for my sensibilities as a civ game. Too much focus is on being the first to make progress not because it advances your civilization but purely for the victory points awarded. To me, it doesn't fit well with the civ theme.

RATING: 7
Resource management in what should be a quick playing game.  Nice ability to play different numbers of players.  VP's once gained are never lost, so game progresses towards an end.  Conflict is a bit pricey and if poorly timed allows others to sweep in behind the attacking force.  Fortunately, conflict is a part, not the whole of the game.  With limited actions per turn, game play is very quick even with large numbers of players minimizing downtime.  But still key decisions to be made when it is time to take a turn.  And if the game continues to play in about 2 hours - happy with the current rating......But more recently, the games seems to be balanced during play to the point where the eventual winner must be decided by that costly combat system (sacking temples) as the other VPs are no longer available.  This results in a drawn out conclusion to the game that really feels inefficient compared to the earlier turns.  If this style of play continues, rating likely to go down to a 6 or 5 as the game just doesn't feel right when played for such a lengthy time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very enjoyable. It was exactly what I hoped it would be: a game with a little more depth than Settlers of Catan, which my family would nevertheless be able to play. The Rondel is a great engine driving the game. Unlike Settlers, where you can sometimes find that you cannot do anything for long stretches because of the unlucky dice rolls, you will always be able to collect and spend resources.

Before playing it I was put off by the supposed lack of interaction at the beginning of the game and the punishing combat rules. Neither of these turned out to be a problem. It is not a game of war or diplomacy, but rather of construction and development. Only a few attacks should take place during the game as you should only attack if you are going to get a concrete benefit (normally a victory card). Certainly, a full-blown war will only weaken the two players involved to the benefit of a third; however, this is not a disadvantage, but rather a feature of the game, channelling the activity of the players towards peaceful expansion and development.

RATING: 9
One of my favorite games, sadly rather hard to set up a game due to how long it takes. Very nice mix of strategies, requires planning in advance, some diplomacy depending on the number of players. Rondel mechanic is great, nearly complete lack of randomness even better.
Most of the game (until late-game maneuver turns) it also progresses rapidly, little downtime.

One thing I don't like is how the game dynamic completely changes depending on number of players. 3 players - barely any interactions and very limited strategy, 4 players almost always devolves into 2 vs 2, 5 players is probably the optimal number and 6 (only one play with 6) is more chaotic with many attacks all around the board.
Could have balanced this better...

RATING: 7
[2011-07-16]
Az első rondellás játék a szerzőtől. Ebben az ókori civilizációk vezetését veszik át a játékosok, városokat, templomokat építenek, ezekből vasat, márványt és aranyat szereznek amikből fejlődhetnek, illetve sereget toborozhatnak. A játék célja az 5 fajta személyiségkártyákból a játékosszámtól függő darab elérése. Ezek adott számú tengeri hódításért, templomépítésért, fejlettségért, városszámért és az ellenség templomainak lerombolásáért járnak. Tetszett, hogy egy személyiségkártya elnyerése után már nem lehet elveszíteni. Szép a játék kivitele, nagyon sok faalkatrész tartozik hozzá. A játékmenet is kellemes, de például nem nagyon éreztem a játék során, hogy érdemes lenne bármilyen lépést is kihagyni a rondellán, illetve bármikor is fizetni azért, hogy többet léphessek az alap 3-nál. Ennél a játéknál is azt éreztem, mindenre menni a legjobb, egysíkú stratégia meglepne ha győzelemre vezetne, de örülnék ha későbbi játékok rám cáfolnának. Az általam eddig próbált másik rondellás Imperial 2030-at hozzám közelebb állónak tartom. Ott is van legalább ennyi konfrontáció mint ebben, sőt lehet még több, de kevésbé nyíltan jelenik meg. Az Antike jó játék, nem is igazán tudok hibát találni benne. A játék talán elnyúlhat és talán páros számúan jó igazán játszani vele, bár hatan talán belassul, de mindenképpen egy korrekt darab.

RATING: 9
I love the rondel!  Really.  That thing is awesome.  The way that the movement around the wheel works is such that you always want to be where you cannot be.  Perfectly, angst entrenched.
 
This is a great civ building game.  I love the number of different goals that you can go for in the game.  This keeps your options open and a flexible player can always find a way to keep themselves in the game.  The use of coins as a universal currency gives players the feeling that everything they do is based on their own decisions, and they are not just trapped by the constraints of the game. 

The victory point system should appeal to many euro-gamers as you won't always need to fight to win, and the game system succumb to bashing the leader.  Neither one of these things bother me, but it means that I can play this with more people.  In this game the threat of attack may be more effective than attack itself.

I'm pleased that the map is double sided. This should allow for the game to feel fresh for a long time to come.

UPDATE: I don't recommend this game for 6 players.  The players in the middle are a bit screwed, which wouldn't be so awful, if there wasn't a deficit of VP's to overextend an stagnant, prolonged, anti-climactic ending.  When all players are doing fairly well, there is a dearth of choices available at game end as all the options for victory are tapped out.  While there are technically enough cards to go around, there just isn't enough incentive for the few who cannot possibly hope to achieve those conditions.  If you play with 6 players I highly recommend you play to 6 victory points.  With a 6 it gets ... a 6.

Update:  I still love the game and I am now convinced after many plays that there is just one too many VP's needed for the win for any number of players.  The last VP can add 30 to 45 minutes, it is nice in that this is usualy where people catch up, but often the struggle leaves one person as the spoiler.  The game already plays close to the bone, why offer less choices at the end...?

UPDATE: Use the official variant.  If you get all of your tech up you get a VP.

RATING: 7
This is NOT a civilization game so much as an empire-building game with some civ-like themes. 

The 2-sided map allows you to play in 2 different settings. Each side shows a number of land and sea regions. All land regions have a city site of 1 of 3 types: if you build a city there, you will be able to generate that resource (marble, iron, or gold). Players start in defined areas, depending on the # playing, controlling 3 cities (1 of each type). The board also shows a Rondel - the defining feature of the game (below). 

At the start of each round, everyone gets a coin (which can be used as any of the other 3 resources). Then, on your turn, you choose your action from the Rondel. This is a circle divided into 8 wedges, 1 for each of the types of action. You may move your token clockwise only; up to 3 spaces for free, then each additional space costs a resource. Actions include:
- 3 production actions (iron, gold, or marble): every city you built of the appropriate type produces 1 of that resource (subject to modifications; temples and some knowledge advances improve production)
- 3 resource-using actions (arming, uses 1 iron/token produced; know-how, uses gold to discover 1 of 8 areas of knowledge; temple building, uses 5 marble/temple)
- 2 maneuver actions (allows ship/legion movement and combat). Every unit has 1 movement point initially; knowledge advances will increase this. Combat only occurs if either player decides not to coexist peacefully, and is very simple -- 1:1 removal of units till only 1 side is left. Conquering a city requires 1 move point.
After action is taken, you may spend 1 of each resource per city you'd like to build (1 per empty city site).

The game ends when a defined VP total is reached (varies with #players). VP are earned by:
- building cities (every 5th --> 1VP)
- building temples (every 3rd --> 1VP)
- building a navy (1VP / 7 sea areas containing your ships)
- know-how (if you're the first to discover 1 of the 8 areas --> 1VP)
- destroying temples (1VP/temple)
There is a limited # of VP available in each of these areas.

The Rondel is very nice - with only 1 thing to do on your turn, things should move quickly. Should - our first game with 6newbies took for EVER! But I need to play some more - not sure how fun this really is -- a 7 may be a bit generous.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Great theme, fast turns, interesting decisions, excellent production.  This is the best civ-building game I've played.

RATING: N/A
We could say that this game has two parts: civilization and fighting.
First of all we have to build a strong civilization. After that, and trying to get your last victory points, you are almost obliged to fight.
I have to say that this is a very clever game with clever mechanics and a very well balanced victory condition.

First rondel series game. It seems to have some balance issues. Not all of the civilizations seem to have the same possibilities to win. I think that Greeks and Arabs are more comfortable on the map an have more time to strength themselves and prepare to the fights. Middle civilizations are more targeted because they naturally occupy strategic positions on the map.

Haven't played it that often but it appears to me that development vp's are key to success. No player should be allowed to get more than 2 of these vp's not only because they are easier to get but also because they actually improve our civilizations making them stronger.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An excellent game that is less of a conquest game than it looks. On the first try, everyone makes the mistake of thinking that the goal is to conquer the map, and each faction gets into a cold war-like game of arming before losing all its units attacking territories and desperately trying to hold them. It's just the wrong way... 

Keep your eye on the cards, don't try to conquer the entire map !

Pros: 
- Each turn goes very fast.
- Nicely balanced.
- Many different ways to victory
- Keeps you on the edge, with a lot of ways of stealing the win at the very end.

CONS:
- Longer than advertised, and might drag a bit.
- NOT a real conquest game, despite the fact that many players want it to be. Armies can at best defend and steal a temple or two. It's not really a flaw, it's just that the game has a deceiving nature...
- Central player has a clear disadvantage, and no one will persuade me of the contrary. Since it pays to get a temple or two, the central player has just more chances of being the target of the other players.

Overall, a very pleasant experience.

RATING: 5
[RGG 2006 edition] {Europe, civilization, economic; fighting; optimal 4-6p} Antike is very typical Euro gamers' game. It's multifaceted and provides tactics and strategies for players. It's luckless - no card, no dice. It has a tech tree but it's not the civilization game that I am looking for.

The design of the game is quite nice - simple and clean. Full of player interaction. It's not a conquest game - military is not the only way to achieve winning. It's balanced that no single winning condition or tech tree is overpowered. However, IMO, it's overly balanced, which makes the game quite dry and abstract. Besides, perfect information is an Achilles' heel of this game. I understand that it's designed to be luckless but this makes everything purely mathematic is very dry to me.

Antike has a crafted game system but I don't see much fun here. I would play Imperial instead, which is better themed.

RATING: 8
Light civilization game with multiple routes to victory. You begin the game with a set number of regions each of which produces a different resource (iron, marble, gold). With the resources you can expand your military power, build temples (which increase a region's resource production), develop a technology or build a city as you colonize greater areas of the map. Choices are pretty straightforward and the technologies are very basic with only one progression form a starting technology to a second more powerful one. Victory is gained by accumulating a set number of cards, which are acquired through numerous ways e.g. conquering certain numbers of regions, building a certain number of temples, developing technologies first etc. Because their isn't one path to victory and you can combine various tactics the game is prevented from going stale with repeated plays and this game is a very good alternative to heavier and lengthier civilization games when available playing time is limited.

RATING: N/A
A good, very solid war-euro. It can be good as a gateway game. Simple rules and strong interaction. There is no luck involved, all the information is visible for all players, so you can always analyse the positions of all your opponents (it can be disadvantage for some people). 
The game can be divided into two parts: (a) development: inventing techs, founding new cities, buying temples and units, (b) short and intensive fight, where you try to gain the remaining VPs. For the first part, there are few combinations of optimal moves (with some variations), so when you are playing with experienced gamers, the core game is only part b.

Number of plays: 2.

RATING: 6.5
Units die in a 1-to-1 ratio so attacking is discouraged. But destroying temples gives you VP and is encouraged. But by the end of the game temples will probably have 5 defense, so it'll take a lot of troops to do this. Building all these troops on his border will alert your opponent and he'll build/move troops. So why do it? Well at that point all the other VP cards are probably taken. So you've got a Civ game that's build/develop your civ and then decide who's the winner by taking temples at the end of the game. What's the points of building/developing your civ then?

It also suffers from the problem where you can see someone is going to win a few turns ahead. This makes for a boring end to the game. (Ok, so I see that you're going to win, so I'll attack this other guys units because there's nothing else for me to complete before the game ends)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played once as a two-player experience. At its heart, Antike is an elaboration on Risk. By adding timing and resource management via the rondel mechanic, the designer was able to add a great deal of interest to the basic formula of area control -- not least of which is a luckless combat system. Nonetheless, the game does seem to bog down in a pattern typical to Risk-like games. There is a tug of war between the players over border territories once all of the neutral areas are claimed. Eventually, one side gathers enough momentum to spread out enough to win; however, that slug-fest in the middle game can become tedious. What I do like is that turns are relatively quick, and that you can win by building infrastructure just as well as by conquest. I look forward to a 3+ player game.

UPDATE: I played again with 3. While there are some problems with the three-player experience (e.g., two players fight while the third runs away with the game), I was nonetheless impressed. The different strategies available are impressive. It's a Risk-like game that delivers a rewarding, Euro-style challenge. I think a four-player match would be superb. I'm upping my rating.

RATING: 7
Antike es un juego de estrategia y evolución de civilizaciones antiguas: Grecia, Roma, Fenicia, Cartago, Persia, etc. Las naciones deben crear ciudades, templos, ejércitos y flotas, pero también deben descubrir nuevos principios de la ciencia y tecnología. El tablero de juego tiene dos caras para dos escenarios distintos. El juego no depende de la suerte de un dado o de las cartas, sino de tus planes y de la diplomacia. Es juego se la Serie Rondel (Navegador, Imperial, etc.)

RATING: 5
I had pretty high hopes for Antike after reading about it, until I saw the Board Games with Scott video, which praised the game, but explained that fighting was relatively punishing so players tended to turtle (i.e., build up in their own corner and avoid conflict).  Games that allow fighting but discourage it are a pet peeve of mine, and I was worried that this would fit that mold.  Unfortunately it did, which made it not as good as I had originally hoped, but it also could be much worse.  I'm pretty ambivalent about the game now.  I like the Rondel mechanic since it makes turns quick and presents some difficult decisions, but combat is almost too simplistic for its own good.  The game also felt split into two parts, with the first being a race to pick up the easy victory points, and then everyone plateaued, at which point whoever could avoid fighting and wait around to pick up the spoils would have the best chance of winning.  I'd be willing to play again, but probably wouldn't be the one to suggest it (and don't feel the need to own it now).

RATING: 8
Just played a four player game of this.  It seems to me that starting in a corner is an advantage.  The two players that only had two fronts to worry about were in the lead with the two players who had three fronts slightly behind.  Apparently there is a thread that discusses this very issue with some disagreement about who has the advantage.  I will have to read that, but perhaps the players with three fronts should have come to an agreement.

I think it works well that the game plays lightning fast during the early development while not much is going on and when it slows down, the level of interaction increases accordingly.  I like that most of the game focuses on development, but most games are going to end in a fight.  It is also a surprisingly simple game.  I used the Board Games with Scott video to explain the rules for me, and only a couple of additional clarifications were needed.

Its likely to be compared with Tempus when that comes out, and while this is a very good game, I think Tempus is a little more interesting and offers greater variety.

RATING: 3.5
This was the first game I've played with the Rondel mechanic.  I felt it was novel but not particularly fun or meaningful in this implementation.  Still willing to try other Rondel game though to see if they are implemented better.

During the entire game, I was wishing I was playing other similar games instead.  NB: this game is NOT a civilization or empire building game.  For comparison, I felt Settlers of Catan was more of civilization or empire building game than Antike and I would hardly say Settlers is particularly a good example of either types of these games.  For the city building and gathering of different resource aspect of the game, Settlers of Catan scratches the itch much better than Antike.

Nor is Antike a combat game.  Antike is actually a VP points race game.  Very much in the way that Nexus Ops is not a straight area control/domination game and is all about VP.  As such a game, I felt Nexus Ops was much stronger and more enjoyable in this regard.  In fact the technologies in Antike are analogous to the abilities offered from the stronger more expensive units and the Energize cards in Nexus Ops.  Nexus Ops also has an excellent balancing mechanism where Antike is prone to run away leaders and king making. 

As a hybrid of Settlers and Nexus Ops, Antike does have its own niche but it wasn't one that I felt was implemented particularly well nor unique enough that it scratched an itch that either Settlers or Nexus Ops couldn't scratch on its own.

I find the gameplay boring and devoid of tension even in face of combat.

RATING: 10
Played a 4 and two 5 player games recently, all with newbie players. Easy to explain rules (although I muffed the city building rules... :( ). Great tension when each "Civ" starts to expand. Great player interaction as folks try to cook-up deals. It's fun when as soon as you've finished playing one game when one or two of the new players say "Let's play again, NOW! and everyone says "YES!!!".....the mark of a grand game IMHO! I played a very enjoyable 4-player SOLO game recently. In that solo game I started one Civ on the Growth strategy, one Civ on the Military strategy, and one on the Progress strategy all mentioned in the FAQ part of the rulebook. The 4th civ I tried a combination growth-military strategy. Great fun!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Not sure what the big deal is with the rondel, I wasn't impressed in Hamburgum, nor was I here.  Everything just seem very scripted.  Round and round you gather up the resources, then you either research for technology, expand your territories, or construct temples so you can get more marble, gold or iron.  At least the beginning rounds moved fast enough not to be too bothersome, but once all the easy VP sources have been exhausted, the game slows down to a crawl as it moves into the conflict phase, because by then you can only gain additional points from attacking your neighbors and sacking their temples.  This is where everything becomes a drag, as the downtime multiplies, especially when you are in a 6-player game like I was, I totally wanted to just concede and call it quits.

Horrible endgame with an overrated system, while I did like the different methods of achieving VP, I just think the rest of the gameplay was poorly implemented.  My first experience with Antike was thoroughly underwhelming.

RATING: 5
It's ok. It's light and fast. The various starting empires don't really have any historical flavour whatsoever other than starting location, nor does the distribution of resources on the game board, which is a drawback and makes it feel a bit dry. The one-for-one annihalation combat can makes a deterministic game even more so, but at least it's fair. Just be very careful with temple placement, practically nothing is safe in the endgame unless its tucked away far from everything.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
